    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="994f9432ed9747b7870e41506e69ca5a"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="994f9432ed9747b7870e41506e69ca5a">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '6860b54b-bb09-4688-bed7-6ac509d72b7a'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d118539424" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>In this work, we report a novel architecture with FeP nanodots fully embedded in nitrogen doped carbon microplates skeleton (FeP@CMS) via the spatial confinement of carbon quantum dots (CQDs) . Such a structure allows the stress transfer between FeP nanodots and self-adaptive carbon matrix in sodium storage, which overcomes the intrinsic chemo-mechanical degradation of metal phosphides during long-term cycling.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9286d053-7ef0-4bbb-abbe-3bbe2c153844/anie202303875-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9286d053-7ef0-4bbb-abbe-3bbe2c153844/anie202303875-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/19c6c3c9-61ca-41d1-865f-0fa4fd819f0f/anie202303875-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9286d053-7ef0-4bbb-abbe-3bbe2c153844/anie202303875-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d118539429" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Transition-metal phosphides (TMPs) as typical conversion-type anode materials demonstrate extraordinary theoretical charge storage capacity for sodium ion batteries, but the unavoidable volume expansion and irreversible capacity loss upon cycling represent their long-standing limitations. Herein we report a stress self-adaptive structure with ultrafine FeP nanodots embedded in dense carbon microplates skeleton (FeP@CMS) via the spatial confinement of carbon quantum dots (CQDs). Such an architecture delivers a record high specific capacity (778 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and ultra-long cycle stability (87.6 % capacity retention after 10 000 cycles at 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>), which outperform the state-of-the-art literature. We decode the fundamental reasons for this unprecedented performance, that such an architecture allows the spontaneous stress transfer from FeP nanodots to the surrounding carbon matrix, thus overcomes the intrinsic chemo-mechanical degradation of metal phosphides.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>

   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202303875-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202303875-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Rechargeable sodium ion batteries (SIBs) as alternatives to LIBs, have received growing attentions due to their cost advantages in the acquisition of raw materials and production processes, which provide similar operating voltage and energy density to lithium ion batteries.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0001_R_d118539416e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Among varieties of anode materials, conversion-type materials especially transition-metal phosphides (TMP) have attracted broad attentions thanks to their salient theoretical capacity, appropriate reaction potential, extensively available resources, and eco-friendliness.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0007_R_d118539416e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, the practical applications of TMPs anodes are hindered by their serious volume expansion during the continuous charge/discharge process thus leading severe chemo-mechanical degradation upon long-term cycling, and the sluggish electronic conductivity of TMPs would generally cause poor rate capability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0011_R_d118539416e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Abundant strategies have been applied to solve the above issues, including reducing the particle size,<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0014_R_d118539416e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nano Energy">4</a></sup></span> manipulating the microstructures of TMPs,<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0015_R_d118539416e620" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span> introducing buffer matrix materials,<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0016_R_d118539416e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - eScience">6</a></sup></span> and so on.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0017_R_d118539416e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></sup></span> For instance, downsizing TMP particles to nano-scale could efficiently shorten the charge transfer length, while introducing a carbon buffer matrix could alleviate the volume expansion and meanwhile increase the overall electronic conductivity. Among various TMPs, iron phosphide (FeP) has been evidenced as a strong competitor due to the abundant Fe and P resources together with its high theoretical capacity (≈926 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0021_R_d118539416e632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - RSC Adv.">8</a></sup></span> Moreover, the suitable Fe−P distances (2.186–2.447 Å) can facilitate the unhampered migration of Na<sup>+</sup> (2.04 Å) within the FeP unit cell.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0022_R_d118539416e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Mater.">9</a></sup></span> A metal–organic framework (MOF) induced FeP composited with carbon nanotube was reported to deliver a maximized rate capability (262 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> @ 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>) known so far, but its cycling stability was unsatisfactory with only 75 % capacity retention after 200 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0023_R_d118539416e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a></sup></span> Some researchers incorporated FeP nanoparticles into nitrogen-doped carbon nanosheets, which delivered a promising cycle life up to 10 000 cycles attributed to the continuous particle refinement upon cycling. However, such long cycle performance was achieved at the expense of the specific capacity (only 90.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> @ 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0024_R_d118539416e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">11</a></sup></span> Therefore, the trade-off between the rate capability/capacity and their long cycle stability represents the main bottleneck for the practical applications for TMPs. To break such long-standing limitations, a rational design of TMPs that can buffer the charge/discharge induced stress without sacrificing the electrochemical performance, as well as the fundamental explanation on the underlying mechanism are urgently demanded.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we investigate a state-of-the-art design with FeP nanodots (≈2–5 nm) embedded in nitrogen-doped carbon microplate skeleton (FeP@CMS) through a carbon quantum dots (CQDs) spatially confined synthesis. Distinguished from conventional compositing or forming a yolk-shell structure with carbon, such an architecture allows the complete embedding of FeP nanodots within the partially graphitized bulk carbon microplates, thus enabling the intimate contacts between the two components. We unveil both theoretically and experimentally, that the graphitic carbon in CMS could share the stress produced by the FeP nanodots, thus alleviating the electrochemo-mechanical coupling effect in the charge/discharge process. As a consequence, the as-prepared FeP@CMS electrode material can simultaneously achieve a remarkable rate performance (778–211 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> @ 0.05–20 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and ultra-long cycle stability with 87.6 % capacity retention after 10 000 cycles at high rate (20 A g<sup>−1</sup>).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202303875-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202303875-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0001">1a</a> illustrates the preparation procedure of the FeP@CMS. In this process, CQDs was prepared by employing ethylenediamine and sodium alginate as raw materials (Figure S1, Supporting Information). Then the as-prepared CQDs were mixed with Fe ions (FeCl<sub>3</sub> solution) and undergo a hydrothermal process, whereby the incompletely carbonized polymer chains on the surface of CQDs can coordinate and chelate with Fe<sup>3+</sup> through rich oxygen functionalities.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0025_R_d118539416e682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">12</a></sup></span> Subsequently, the as obtained Fe/CQDs precursor was freeze dried and pyrolyzed under argon atmosphere, followed by phosphating using sodium hypophosphite (NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub>) as phosphorus source to get FeP@CMS. Unlike other carbon precursors, CQDs derived carbon doesn't undergo significant structural evolution during the pyrolysis process, thereby the carbonized core in the CQDs can prevent the agglomerate of the FeP particles, and enable the uniform distribution and confined growth of FeP nanodots.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0026_R_d118539416e690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Chem.">13</a></sup></span> The structural evolution of FeP@CMS at different synthesis stage was monitored by the transmission electron microscopy (TEM, Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0001">1b</a>–<a href="#anie202303875-fig-0001">d</a>). Moreover, the reaction mechanism of functional groups during heat treatment was further verified by Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR, Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0001">1e</a>). It shows that the oxygen-containing groups of the precursors gradually decrease after freeze-drying and subsequent heat treatment, implying that functional groups are compounded during the synthesis process. Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0001">1f</a> illustrates the possible bonding sites of the functional groups.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202303875-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6d58b087-d13a-4d45-9344-109010aaa180/anie202303875-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6d58b087-d13a-4d45-9344-109010aaa180/anie202303875-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4a1b47a7-3eb4-4520-823b-dc28970edf3a/anie202303875-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6d58b087-d13a-4d45-9344-109010aaa180/anie202303875-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202303875-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202303875" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis process of the FeP@CMS material. TEM images of the b) CQDs, c) Fe/CQDs precursor, and d) FeP@CMS. e) FTIR spectra of the FeP@CMS, Fe/CQDs and CQDs. f) Schematic illustration of the possible chemical reaction sites of CQDs.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The morphology of FeP@CMS was characterized by the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0002">2a</a>) and TEM images (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0002">2b</a>). Compared with that of FeP@RGO (≈10 nm) and FeP (≈50 nm, Figure S2a–d, Supporting Information), the FeP nanodots (≈2–5 nm) on FeP@CMS demonstrates the smallest particle size and dense distribution. The high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0002">2c</a> and <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0002">d</a>) further confirmed the size of FeP nanodots (≈2–5 nm), and the lattice distance of 0.188 nm corresponds to the (211) plane. The FeP nanodots were observed to be well fixed in the carbon substrate, demonstrating that CQDs are feasible for confining the growth and aggregation of FeP particles, which was further verified through acid washing (Figure S3a and S3b, Supporting Information). The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0002">2e</a>) were indexed to (020), (211), (111) planes of FeP. High-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) image and the energy dispersive spectrometer (EDS) elemental mapping images were photographed (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0002">2f</a>–<a href="#anie202303875-fig-0002">j</a>) to further confirm the uniform distribution of FeP nanodots in CMS. X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of the FeP@CMS (Figure S4, Supporting Information) shows distinct Bragg diffraction peaks corresponding to FeP (JCPDS No. 65-2595, <i>Pnma</i> (62)), and the broad diffraction peak centered at ≈20.95° proves the existence of carbon composition. HRTEM images (Figure S5, Supporting Information) further proved the existance of small graphitization areas around the FeP nanoparticles, with the graphite crystal spacing ≥0.4 nm. The mass fraction of FeP in the composite was evaluated by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) as 63.8 wt % (Figure S6, Supporting Information). Raman spectra investigation was also carried out (Figure S7, Supporting Information), and the intensity ratio (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) is calculated as 0.88, indicating the partially ordered feature of the carbon substrate. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) survey spectrum (Figure S8a, Supporting Information) confirms the co-existence of C, N, O, Fe, P elements in FeP@CMS. The presence of N element originated from ethylenediamine, and the chemical state of N element can be determined according to the N1s spectrum (Figure S8b, Supporting Information). The high-resolution C1s spectrum (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0002">2k</a>) shows the primary sp<sup>3</sup>/sp<sup>2</sup> carbonaceous peak (284.7 eV) and the C−N (286.9 eV) and O−C=O (289.3 eV) peaks. Notably, a low-BE peak signal at 283.8 eV was detected that represent the typical Fe−C bonding.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0027_R_d118539416e768" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">14</a></sup></span> The peak at 285.5 eV can be assigned as C−P bonding, confirming the strong interactions between the FeP nanodots and CMS.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0028_R_d118539416e771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">15</a></sup></span> In the P2p spectrum (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0002">2l</a>), the peaks present at 129.47 and 130.34 eV correspond to the P2p<sub>1/2</sub> and the P2p<sub>3/2</sub> of P−Fe bond, respectively, which reveals the formation of FeP. The strong peak signal at high-BE was deconvoluted into three different sub-peaks at 133.23, 134.2 and 134.56 eV, which can be ascribed to the P−C, P−O and P=O bonds, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0029_R_d118539416e782" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">16</a>, <a href="#anie202303875-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0030_R_d118539416e782" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Res.">17</a></sup></span> The Fe2p spectrum shows two peaks at 712.49 and 725.3 eV corresponding to the Fe<sup>3+</sup>2p<sub>3/2</sub> and the Fe<sup>3+</sup>2p<sub>1/2</sub> of the oxidized Fe species, along with one satellite peak located at 730.7 eV (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0002">2m</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0024_R_d118539416e797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">11</a></sup></span> Moreover, the peaks at 707.89 and 720.72 eV can be ascribed to the Fe2p<sub>3/2</sub> and the Fe2p<sub>1/2</sub>, which are the characteristic peaks of FeP.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0031_R_d118539416e804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18">18</a></sup></span> It is worth noting that a weak peak at 710.15 eV is observed, which can be attributed to the Fe−C bond formed between FeP and CMS.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0036_R_d118539416e808" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Nano">19</a></sup></span> In contrast, these signal peaks of Fe−C bond and P−C bond are not found in the high-resolution XPS spectrum of pure FeP (Figure S9a and S9b, Supporting Information).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202303875-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4652320b-77c3-4ede-9726-199ea6cfb6b2/anie202303875-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4652320b-77c3-4ede-9726-199ea6cfb6b2/anie202303875-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/952d6ca6-8017-454b-9803-04897eda8f80/anie202303875-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4652320b-77c3-4ede-9726-199ea6cfb6b2/anie202303875-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202303875-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202303875" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) SEM image of the FeP@CMS. b) TEM and c), d) HRTEM images of the FeP@CMS (FeP are indicated with a yellow dashed line). e) SAED pattern of the FeP@CMS. f)–j) HAADF-STEM image matched with the corresponding EDS mapping images of FeP@CMS. k)–m) High resolution XPS spectra and the corresponding fitted curves of C1s, P2p and Fe2p for FeP@CMS.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performances of FeP@CMS and the control groups were tested on a half-cell system using button cell (CR2032) within the voltage range from 0.01 to 3 V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup> wtih sodium foil and FeP@CMS coated on Cu foil as the counter and working electrode respectively. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurement was carried out at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>−1</sup> to investigate the electrochemical behavior of FeP@CMS (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0003">3a</a>) during charge/discharge process. In the first cathodic scan, the broad peak located at ≈0.95 V is related to the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film. And the sharp peak located at ≈0.15 should be attributed to the intermediate Na<sup>+</sup> sodiation process of FeP+<i>x</i> Na<sup>+</sup>+<i>x</i> e<sup>−</sup>→Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP and a followed conversion reaction of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP+(3−<i>x</i>) Na+(3−<i>x</i>) e<sup>−</sup>→Na<sub>3</sub>P+Fe to form nano-sized Fe particles embedded in the Na<sub>3</sub>P matrix.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0036_R_d118539416e872" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Nano">19</a></sup></span> For the anodic scan, the strong peak centered at ≈1.8 V is related to the Na<sup>+</sup> deconversion reaction from Na<sub>3</sub>P to FeP. For the subsequent cycles, the cathodic peak shifted to higher potential (≈1.1 V), suggesting an improved electrode reaction kinetics, which is probably caused by lattice activation of FeP during sodiation/desodiation process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0036_R_d118539416e880" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Nano">19</a></sup></span> The well-overlapped redox peaks in the following cycles demonstrate a highly reversible Na<sup>+</sup> storage process of the FeP@CMS electrode, which was not observed in the control groups (Figure S10, Supporting Information). In addition, it can be seen from the first three cycles of charge/discharge curves that FeP@CMS shows the highest initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) compared to the control groups (Figure S11, Supporting Information). The charge/discharge profiles of FeP@CMS, FeP@RGO, and FeP at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> in Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0003">3b</a> further illustrate the capacity advantage of FeP@CMS (724 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) over FeP@RGO (459 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and FeP (264 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> and lower medium discharge voltage (≈0.7 V) of FeP@CMS, in full battery applications. Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0003">3c</a>&amp;d illustrate the most superior rate performance of FeP@CMS electrode over FeP@RGO and FeP, which shows average charge capacities of 709, 663, 616, 541, 443, 378, 324, 250 and 211 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, and 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Table S1, Supporting Information). Impressively, the FeP@CMS electrode demonstrates a record high specific capacity of 184 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 10 000 cycles at a current density of 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, with a capacity retention rate of 87.6 % (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0003">3e</a>). And the corresponding charge/discharge profiles (Figure S12, Supporting Information) demonstrate that the shape of the charge/discharge curves at different cycles are basically consistent, further confirming the long cycle stability of the FeP@CMS electrode. Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0003">3f</a> shows a winning cycling performance of FeP@CMS (414 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2000 cycles with no capacity decay) over FeP@RGO and FeP electrodes at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Such high electrochemical performance surpasses the state-of-the-art FeP based composites and other metal phosphide anode materials for SIBs reported in literatures (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0003">3g</a> and <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0003">h</a>; Table S2 and S3, Supporting Information).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202303875-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b42a11aa-387a-43e1-ad64-ed3643615035/anie202303875-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b42a11aa-387a-43e1-ad64-ed3643615035/anie202303875-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f430870a-e596-42c4-8982-25500fbc194a/anie202303875-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b42a11aa-387a-43e1-ad64-ed3643615035/anie202303875-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202303875-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202303875" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) The 1st-5th cyclic voltammetry curves of FeP@CMS. b) The charge/discharge profiles of FeP@CMS, FeP@RGO, and FeP at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Rate performance of FeP@CMS, FeP@RGO and FeP electrodes at different current densities. d) The charge/discharge profiles of FeP@CMS at different current densities. e) The cycling performance of FeP@CMS at 20 A g<sup>−1</sup> (after initial 5 cycles at 0.05 A g−1). f) The cycling performance of FeP@CMS, FeP@RGO, and FeP electrodes at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (after initial 5 cycles at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>). g) Comparison of the rate performance with other metal phosphide anode materials for SIBs. h) Comparison of the cycling performance with reported FeP based anode materials for SIBs. i) Nyquist plots of the FeP@CMS, FeP@RGO, and FeP (inset: equivalent circuit used for fitting the experimental data). j) Nyquist plots of FeP@CMS at different cycles.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To gain insight into the outstanding electrochemical performance of FeP@CMS, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was performed and the data were fitted by the inset equivalent circuit (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0003">3i</a>). The FeP@CMS electrode demonstrates the smallest <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and <i>R<sub>s</sub></i> values thanks to the unique structure of carbon microplates skeleton. Moreover, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0003">3j</a>, <i>R<sub>f</sub></i> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of FeP@CMS show a rapid decrease in the first 100 cycles and then remained stable in the subsequent 1000–10 000 cycles, and the initial resistance decrease should be related to the gradual wetting of the electrode surface with electrolyte and the continuous formation of SEI.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0037_R_d118539416e983" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Storage Mater.">20</a></sup></span> With cycling, the sufficient pervasion of the electrolyte leads to the formation of the completely firm SEI, resulting in a stable resistance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0034_R_d118539416e986" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18c - J. Power Sources">18c</a></sup></span> The fitted <i>R<sub>s</sub></i>, <i>R<sub>f</sub></i>, and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values based on EIS measurements are shown in Table S4 (Supporting Information). Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was also carried out to manifest the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion behavior of the samples. A series of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/92c0f386-105a-48c9-a1f1-ccbc4c8a00e2/anie202303875-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
) were calculated as shown in Figure S13 (Supporting Information), which indicate that the average log<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/72ffb4ee-92cd-4df1-a302-d5b932fc16ee/anie202303875-math-0002.png"></span><math></math>
 of FeP@CMS (−9.42) is an order of magnitude higher than that of FeP@RGO (−10.29) and FeP (−10.47). In addition, we studied the FeP@CMS electrodes′ kinetics to separate the surface-controlled capacitive capacity and diffusion-controlled capacity by analyzing the CV data at various scan rates from 0.2–2 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S14a and S14b, Supporting Information). And the faster surface-controlled capacitive behavior is proved by the bigger <i>b</i>-value of FeP@CMS (0.88 and 0.76) compared to that of FeP@RGO (0.81 and 0.71) (Figure S14c and S14d, Supporting Information). Moreover, the capacitive contributions of FeP@CMS were calculated to be 68–96 % at different scan rates, which is higher than that of FeP@RGO (Figure S15a–d, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202303875-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202303875-sec-0003-title"> Full battery performance</h3>
            
            <p>A full-cell was assembled using NVP@C (Figure S16, Supporting Information) as the cathode and FeP@CMS as the anode to evaluate the practical application of FeP@CMS as schematically illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0004">4a</a>. The as-assembled SIB full-cell shows a high ICE ≈88.7 % at 0.2 C at the voltage window of 1.2–3.6 V (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0004">4b</a>) and outstanding average rate capability of 106.15, 80.22, 69.87, 62.86, and 51.39 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (based on the total active mass of both electrodes) at 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 C, respectively (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0004">4c</a> and <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0004">d</a>). More importantly, the full-cell demonstrated an excellent cycling performance with 90.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> capacity retained after 100 cycles at 0.2 C (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0004">4e</a>). As a result, the as-constructed full-cell delivered an energy density of 193–117 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at the power density of 114–2867 W kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the total active mass of both electrodes), which is higher than those previously reported FeP-based SIBs full-cells (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0004">4f</a>; Table S5, Supporting Information).
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202303875-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b873e268-63cb-42ff-a3db-1dd728f5cd7d/anie202303875-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b873e268-63cb-42ff-a3db-1dd728f5cd7d/anie202303875-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ef5d9705-b9dc-4459-9240-ab45b245464a/anie202303875-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b873e268-63cb-42ff-a3db-1dd728f5cd7d/anie202303875-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202303875-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202303875" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>a) The schematic illustration of FeP@CMS/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C full-cell (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> denoted as NVP, with a theoretical capacity of 117 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). b) The 1<sup>st</sup>–5<sup>th</sup> charge/discharge profiles of the FeP@CMS/NVP@C full-cell between 1.2–3.6 V at 0.2 C (1 C=117 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). c) The rate performance of the FeP@CMS/NVP@C full-cell. d) The charge/discharge profiles of the FeP@CMS/NVP@C full-cell at different rates. e) Cycling performance of FeP@CMS/NVP@C full-cell at 0.2 C (after initial 5 cycles). f) Comparison of full-cell Ragone plots of the FeP@CMS/NVP@C full-cell and previous FeP-based SIB full-cells.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202303875-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202303875-sec-0004-title"> Charge Storage Mechanism Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>To unveil the structural origin of the superior cycle stability of FeP@CMS, density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed to simulate the sodiation process of Na<sup>+</sup> in FeP cluster, FeP@RGO, and FeP@CMS, respectively (Figure S17, Supporting Information). A representative monolayer graphene model of 7×7 supercell was considered as RGO, and a trilayer graphene model with interlayer distance of 0.34 nm has been built to simulate the graphitic carbon area of CMS substrate.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0038_R_d118539416e1143" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Storage Mater.">21</a></sup></span> Intuitively, a direct consequence of bearing stress in FeP and carbon substrate (both CMS and RGO) would be the change on their lattice and bond length, which in return makes these parameters good criteria to measure the stress. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0005">5a</a>, the Fe−P bond lengths in different models were measured and summarized. To discuss the variation trend, the average Fe−P bond lengths are calculated and compared. When wrapped with CMS, the average Fe−P bond length remains almost constant from 2.217 Å for FeP cluster to 2.218 Å for 3 Na binding with a slight increase of only 0.04 % (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0005">5b</a>). This might be attributed to that with the sodiation of FeP nanodots, the bond-length change of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP would produce a push stress on the surrounded carbon substrate, meanwhile the rigid carbon substrate would produce an extrusion stress counterpart, therefore, the average Fe−P bond length could maintain the original bond-length by the rigid wrapping of the combined carbon zone. By contrast, the stretching of Fe−P bond would not be alleviated with RGO substrate whereby the average Fe−P bond increased by 0.54 % from 2.218 Å to 2.230 Å (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0005">5c</a>), which is over 10 times more than that of FeP@CMS. This phenomenon is even more obvious in pure FeP cluster, the average Fe−P bond length extension is as large as 0.77 % with the sodiation process (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0005">5d</a>), that is, the average Fe−P bond length is 2.208 Å in pure FeP cluster, which is enlarged to 2.225 Å for 3 Na inserted. It is worth noting that the average Na−P bond length gradually decreases and stabilizes during the sodiation process for all the samples (Figure S18a–c, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the C=C bond length in the vicinity of the FeP particles (red color) and away from the FeP particles (green color) for both FeP@CMS and FeP@RGO were also analyzed. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0005">5e</a> and <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0005">f</a>, the average bond length and bond length range in the green region have no significant changes in different sodiation states for both FeP@CMS and FeP@RGO. And the average C=C bond lengths of FeP@CMS in red regions are 1.433 Å (0 Na) and 1.432 Å (3 Na), which do not change significantly. However, the bond length range in red region shows a clear narrowing trend, varying from 1.376–1.530 Å for bare FeP@CMS and 1.383–1.501 Å for FeP@CMS with 3 Na. On the contrary, the range of the bond length data in red region for FeP@RGO are 1.416–1.449 Å (0 Na) and 1.414–1.446 Å (3 Na), respectively, which do not show such obvious narrowing trend. This underlines that a self-adaptive change in the C=C bond length occurs in CMS, that is, with the entry of Na, the surrounding C=C bonds would be adaptively shortened or elongated to preserves the lattice stability of FeP nanodots in different directions during the (de)sodiation process. Moreover, it is noticed that the average P−C and Fe−C in FeP@CMS bond lengths increase 2.4 % and 0.5 % (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0005">5g</a> and <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0005">h</a>), respectively. This suggests the P−C bond can actually serve as a bridge that transfer the stress generated in the sodiation/desodiation process from FeP to the self-adaptive carbon substrate. However, it is observed the average Fe−C bond length decreased 0.18 % for FeP@RGO (Figure S18d, Supporting Information), indicating no effective stress transfer path formed between RGO and FeP nanoparticles. Therefore, the underlying mechanism of FeP@CMS can be summarized as follows: The stress generated by the volumetric change of FeP nanodots during the sodiation process can be efficiently transferred to the rigid carbon substrates through the P−C bonds, and dissipated by the relaxation of the self-adaptive C=C bond, thus eventually suppress the intrinsic chemo-mechanical degradation of FeP nanoparticles.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202303875-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f15717ff-2cbc-405f-abca-24100c649a01/anie202303875-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f15717ff-2cbc-405f-abca-24100c649a01/anie202303875-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/905e4a1a-5ba5-4d3a-ba80-e23186126f07/anie202303875-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f15717ff-2cbc-405f-abca-24100c649a01/anie202303875-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202303875-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202303875" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>a) Optimized supercell structures of 3Na bonded system on FeP cluster, FeP@RGO, and FeP@CMS models. b)–d) The statistical Fe−P bond lengths of FeP@CMS, FeP cluster, and FeP@RGO system at different sodiation states. e), f) C=C bond lengths of the FeP@CMS and FeP@RGO systems at different sodiation states. g) P−C bond lengths of the FeP@CMS system at different sodiation states. h) Fe−C bond lengths of the FeP@CMS system at different sodiation states. Ex situ ATR-FTIR patterns of i) FeP@CMS and j) FeP@RGO during the first cycle at a current density of 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>. k) Schematic diagram of stress transfer mechanism of FeP@CMS.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>To further verify this speculation, ex situ ATR-FTIR (attenuated total reflectance Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy) was employed to evidence the stress evolution in FeP@CMS and FeP@RGO. It shows that, the C=C asymmetric stretching vibration characteristic peaks of the FeP@CMS (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0005">5i</a>) (IR (C=C): ν as <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b0c1be2a-ed87-46af-b3b9-678c933db3e3/anie202303875-math-0003.png"></span><math></math>
1510–1560 (br) cm<sup>−1</sup>) first moved to the high wave number (discharging), and then returned to the original position to the low wave number (charging), indicating the enhanced C−C bond energy during discharging and weakened bond energy during charging process. On the one hand, higher bond energy means more stable bond strength,<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0039_R_d118539416e1218" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Comput. Theor. Chem.">22</a></sup></span> on the other hand, the vibration absorption frequency of chemical bond in the infrared spectrum tends to higher wave number with the increase of stress applied on the material.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0040_R_d118539416e1221" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23">23</a></sup></span> Therefore, it can be inferred that the internal stress of the C=C bond in the CMS material first increased and then decreased along with the discharge and charge process. While no similar phenomenon was observed on the peaks of IR(C=C): νas <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c334805d-10b3-499b-88da-378b545f1e86/anie202303875-math-0004.png"></span><math></math>
1600 (br) cm<sup>−1</sup> in FeP@RGO (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0005">5j</a>). This result is consistent with the DFT calculations. A schematic diagram of the electrochemical kinetic process is presented in Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0005">5k</a>.</p>
            
            <p>The SEM images of the electrode surfaces of different samples after different cycles were obtained to further explore the ultra-long cycling stability of FeP@CMS materials. It can be seen that the FeP@CMS electrode surface remains intact without obvious morphological change even after 10 000 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0006">6a</a>–<a href="#anie202303875-fig-0006">c</a>), indicating that the structure is stable and swelling resistant. On the contrary, the FeP@RGO electrode showed obvious pulverization after 1000 cycles, and severe cracks and electrode material peeling off occurred at 2000 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0006">6d</a>–<a href="#anie202303875-fig-0006">f</a>). The structure fragmentation for the FeP electrode was even worse, which almost peeled off from the copper collector surface after 800 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202303875-fig-0006">6g</a>–<a href="#anie202303875-fig-0006">i</a>). This is mainly due to the repeated massive volume change of FeP partical during the continuous de/sodiation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303875-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303875-bib-0043_R_d118539416e1266" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24">24</a></sup></span> Moreover, the ex situ XRD also reflected the better recoverability of FeP nanodots in CMS over FeP nanoparticles on RGO during charge and discharge (Figure S19, Supporting Information). The morphology evolution of the FeP@CMS composite at 100, 1000, and 10 000 cycles was investigated by TEM and SEM (Figure S20, Supporting Information). No obvious change on the FeP nanodot size and the microplate structure was observed even after 10 000 cycles, which is consistent with the calculation results.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202303875-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fe1c5f39-b7e0-4bbb-b05e-4f3bd4f992c9/anie202303875-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/fe1c5f39-b7e0-4bbb-b05e-4f3bd4f992c9/anie202303875-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/83de828f-36c1-4751-a0b3-e9a3a3da677e/anie202303875-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fe1c5f39-b7e0-4bbb-b05e-4f3bd4f992c9/anie202303875-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202303875-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202303875" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>SEM images of the FeP@CMS (a–c), FeP@RGO (d–f), and FeP (g–i) electrodes at different cycles, respectively (The inset images are corresponding digital photographs).</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>In addition, FeP-n and FeP-n@RGO with reduced FeP particle size was prepared by adding oleyl amine to explore the effect of particle size on the electrochemical performance. The corresponding XRD patterns of FeP-n and FeP-n@RGO (Figure S21, Supporting Information) can be indexed to FeP (JCPDS: 65-2595), and the broad diffraction peak centered at ≈26° of FeP-n@RGO proves the existence of RGO. The TEM images (Figure S22, Supporting Information) demonstrate the overall particle size of FeP-n and FeP-n@RGO is about ≈5 nm. The electrochemical performance data (Figure S23, Supporting Information) indicates that the reduced particle size of FeP is beneficial to the reversible capacity for the initial sodiation/desodiation process and slightly improves the rate performance (514–55 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 402–21 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for FeP-n@RGO and FeP-n@ 0.05–20 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and cycle stability (capacity retention rate is 85.5 % and 69 % for FeP-n@RGO and FeP-n after 1000 cycles@ 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>), which are however much inferior to that of the stress self-adaptive structured metal phosphides (FeP@CMS, 709–211 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> @ 0.05–20 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 100 % capacity retained after 2000 cycles@ 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>).</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202303875-sec-0005">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202303875-sec-0005-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, a novel FeP@CMS structure was successfully prepared, taking advantage of the space confinement of carbon quantum dots, whereby the FeP nanodots with an average size of ≈2–5 nm were completely embedded in the dense rigid carbon. We discover through ex situ TEM and SEM that, the FeP@CMS structure shows significantly enhanced anti-chemo-mechanical degradation ability upon cycling, with morphology almost unchanged even after 10 000 cycles. We unveil through DFT calculations and ATR-FTIR analysis, that the P−C bond induced stress transfer from FeP to the adjacent CMS and the stress self-adaptive characteristics underlie the fundamental origin for the suppressed chemo-mechanical degradation upon cycling. As a consequence, the FeP@CMS exhibits outstanding electrochemical sodium storage properties in terms of high specific capacity of 778 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, rapid rate response up to 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and exceptional battery life of 10 000 cycles. This work is no doubt instructive for building up an anti-chemo-mechanical alloy-type or conversion-type anode material for durable sodium ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

            <h2><a href="#" class="accordion__control" role="button" data-references="/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?widgetId=5cf4c79f-0ae9-4dc5-96ce-77f62de7ada9&amp;ajax=true&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202303875&amp;pbContext=%3Bpage%3Astring%3AArticle%2FChapter+View%3BrequestedJournal%3Ajournal%3A15213773%3Bctype%3Astring%3AJournal+Content%3Bwebsite%3Awebsite%3Apericles%3Bjournal%3Ajournal%3A15213773a%3Bwgroup%3Astring%3APublication+Websites%3Bissue%3Aissue%3Adoi%5C%3A10.1002%2Fanie.v62.29%3BpageGroup%3Astring%3APublication+Pages%3Barticle%3Aarticle%3Adoi%5C%3A10.1002%2Fanie.202303875%3BsubPage%3Astring%3AFull+Text&amp;widgetKey=ux3-publicationContent-widget_5cf4c79f-0ae9-4dc5-96ce-77f62de7ada9_3067_4931_139109_164484_en&amp;accordionHeadingWrapper=h2&amp;onlyLog=true" aria-expanded="false"><span class="section__title" id="references-section-1">References</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a></h2>
